William Shakespeare (1564–1616) wrote 37 plays and 154 sonnets that have not been surpassed in any language. Ben Jonson was his great rival and friend. Edmund Spenser's The Faerie Queene (1590–96) is the longest poem in English. Philip Sidney's Astrophil and Stella (1591) introduced the sonnet sequence. John Donne's metaphysical poetry fused erotic desire and theological argument in ways that shocked contemporaries and fascinated T.S. Eliot 300 years later.
Shakespeare's Globe Theatre (rebuilt 1997 on the South Bank) performed plays with no artificial lighting, minimal sets, and an audience that stood, ate, talked and heckled. Modern reconstructions suggest this produced extraordinary performances.

06

The 17th Century — Revolution and Restoration
John Milton (1608–1674) was the greatest English poet after Shakespeare — Paradise Lost (1667) is the English epic, telling the story of Satan's fall and humanity's expulsion from Eden in blank verse of extraordinary power. Milton was a Puritan, a supporter of the English Revolution and Cromwell, who dictated Paradise Lost after going blind. The Restoration (1660) brought John Dryden's satirical verse and the Restoration comedy — sexually explicit, socially cynical plays (Wycherley, Congreve) that shocked later generations. John Bunyan's The Pilgrim's Progress (1678) — a Protestant allegory — was the most widely read book in English after the Bible for two centuries. In Scotland, James Graham, Marquess of Montrose wrote some of the finest lyric poetry in Scots.
Paradise Lost was sold to a publisher for £5 (with an option on three further editions if the first sold out). The first edition of 1,300 copies sold out in 18 months.

07

The 18th Century — The Age of Reason and the Novel
The 18th century invented the modern novel. Daniel Defoe's Robinson Crusoe (1719) — usually cited as the first English novel — tells the story of a man alone on an island, sustained by reason and Protestant enterprise. Samuel Richardson's Pamela (1740) introduced the epistolary novel and the domestic interior. Henry Fielding's Tom Jones (1749) added comic energy and authorial irony. Laurence Sterne's Tristram Shandy (1759–67) anticipated postmodernism by 200 years — a novel that subverts every novelistic convention it adopts. In poetry, Alexander Pope perfected the heroic couplet; Thomas Gray's Elegy Written in a Country Churchyard (1751) is the most quoted poem in English. The Scottish Enlightenment produced David Hume (philosophy), Adam Smith (economics) and James Boswell's biography of Dr Johnson — the greatest biography in the language.
Samuel Johnson's Dictionary (1755) took nine years to compile and contained 42,773 words with 114,000 quotations. Johnson defined a lexicographer as 'a harmless drudge.'

08

Romanticism (1785–1830)
British Romanticism was a revolution against Enlightenment reason, industrialisation and poetic convention. In poetry: William Blake (visionary, engraved his own books, saw angels in Peckham Rye), William Wordsworth and Samuel Taylor Coleridge (Lyrical Ballads, 1798 — the manifesto of English Romanticism), Lord Byron (scandalous, brilliant, died fighting for Greek independence), Percy Bysshe Shelley (atheist, radical, drowned off Italy aged 29), John Keats (died of tuberculosis aged 25, having written the greatest odes in English in a single year). In prose: Mary Shelley's Frankenstein (1818) invented science fiction. In Scotland: Robert Burns wrote in Scots as a political act; Sir Walter Scott invented the historical novel.
Keats wrote his six great odes — Ode to a Nightingale, Ode on a Grecian Urn, To Autumn, and others — in a single year (1819). He had less than two years to live. He asked that his gravestone read: 'Here lies one whose name was writ in water.'

09

The Victorian Novel (1830–1900)
The 19th century was the great age of the English novel. Charles Dickens (1812–1870) published in serial form — Oliver Twist, David Copperfield, Bleak House, Great Expectations — combining social protest with sentimentality and comedy. William Makepeace Thackeray's Vanity Fair (1848) is sharper, more cynical. George Eliot (Mary Ann Evans, 1819–1880) — Middlemarch (1871) is her masterpiece and, many argue, the greatest English novel. Thomas Hardy wrote of rural Dorset destroyed by modernity — Tess of the d'Urbervilles, Jude the Obscure. In poetry: Alfred Lord Tennyson and Robert Browning dominated; Gerard Manley Hopkins invented a compressed, sprung-rhythm poetry not published until after his death. The Brontës — Charlotte (Jane Eyre), Emily (Wuthering Heights), Anne — wrote from the Yorkshire Moors with a passion that their contemporaries found disturbing.
George Eliot wrote Middlemarch while living with George Henry Lewes, a married man she could not legally marry. Victorian society was divided on whether to receive her. Virginia Woolf called Middlemarch 'one of the few English novels written for grown-up people.'

10

Late Victorian and Edwardian — Transitions (1880–1914)
The late Victorian period produced an extraordinary variety. Oscar Wilde (Irish, London-based) combined aesthetic brilliance with social satire — The Importance of Being Earnest, The Picture of Dorian Gray — before being imprisoned for homosexuality in 1895. Robert Louis Stevenson (Scottish) gave the world Treasure Island and Jekyll and Hyde. Arthur Conan Doyle (Scottish) created Sherlock Holmes. Rudyard Kipling wrote The Jungle Book and the poetry of empire. H.G. Wells invented scientific romance — The Time Machine, The War of the Worlds. Joseph Conrad (Polish-born, English-language) wrote Heart of Darkness (1899) — perhaps the most influential novella in English. Thomas Hardy gave up fiction after Jude the Obscure was condemned as immoral and spent his remaining 30 years writing poetry.
Oscar Wilde's imprisonment destroyed him. Released in 1897, he went to France and died three years later. His last words were reportedly: 'Either that wallpaper goes, or I do.'

11

Modernism (1910–1939)
Modernism was a rupture — the conviction that traditional forms could no longer represent modern experience after the First World War. T.S. Eliot (American-born, naturalised British) published The Waste Land (1922) — fragmented, allusive, multilingual, the defining poem of the 20th century. Virginia Woolf (1882–1941) dissolved the boundaries of the novel — Mrs Dalloway, To the Lighthouse, The Waves — creating prose that moves like consciousness. James Joyce (Irish) published Ulysses (1922) — written in London, Paris and Zurich, it retells Homer's Odyssey in a single Dublin day, with a style that changes every chapter. D.H. Lawrence wrote about sex, class and the body with a directness that got him banned. W.B. Yeats (Irish) developed from Celtic mysticism to one of the greatest modern poets. In Scotland, Hugh MacDiarmid launched the Scottish Renaissance — writing in Scots as Gaelic, not dialect.
Eliot brought a draft of around 800 lines to Ezra Pound, who cut it to the 433 lines we know. Eliot himself wrote: “thank God he reduced a mess of some eight hundred lines to about half its size.” Eliot dedicated the published poem to Pound: “il miglior fabbro” — the better craftsman.

12

Inter-War and the 1930s
The 1930s produced socially conscious literature responding to economic depression and the rise of fascism. W.H. Auden's poetry dominated the decade — political, witty, technically brilliant. George Orwell (1903–1950) began documenting working-class England: Down and Out in Paris and London, The Road to Wigan Pier, Homage to Catalonia. Evelyn Waugh satirised the upper class with savage precision — Decline and Fall, Vile Bodies, Brideshead Revisited. Graham Greene wrote Catholic-inflected thrillers of moral ambiguity. In Wales, the poets of the 1930s — Dylan Thomas among them — began their careers. In Scotland, Lewis Grassic Gibbon's Sunset Song (1932) is the greatest Scottish novel of the century.
George Orwell was shot through the throat by a fascist sniper while fighting in Spain. He survived and wrote Homage to Catalonia about the experience. His larynx was permanently damaged.

13

Post-War Literature (1945–1970)
The Second World War produced relatively little great literature during the conflict but enormous amounts after it. George Orwell's Nineteen Eighty-Four (1949) — written while dying of tuberculosis — and Animal Farm (1945) are the great political parables of the century. Samuel Beckett (Irish, writing in both French and English) stripped theatre to its existential core — Waiting for Godot (1953) is perhaps the most performed play in the world. The Angry Young Men — John Osborne, Kingsley Amis, Alan Sillitoe — brought working-class energy and provincial frustration. Philip Larkin (1922–1985) wrote the poetry of provincial England — Hull, missed chances, mortality — with dry precision. Ted Hughes brought animal violence and mythic intensity.
Waiting for Godot was rejected by every major theatre in London before Peter Hall agreed to stage it at the Arts Theatre Club in 1955. It has been performed continuously ever since.

14

The 1960s–80s — Expansion and Diversity
Muriel Spark (Scottish) — The Prime of Miss Jean Brodie (1961) — wit, Catholicism, Edinburgh. Iris Murdoch — philosophy in novel form, 26 novels in 30 years. J.G. Ballard — science fiction as social diagnosis — Crash, The Atrocity Exhibition. V.S. Naipaul (Trinidadian-British) — A House for Mr Biswas, The Enigma of Arrival — won the Nobel Prize in 2001. Doris Lessing (Zimbabwean-British) — The Golden Notebook — won the Nobel in 2007. The Booker Prize (founded 1969) began to define what 'British' literary fiction meant. Northern Ireland produced its great literary generation: Seamus Heaney, Michael Longley, Derek Mahon, Brian Friel — navigating the Troubles with formal precision.
Doris Lessing was told by publishers in the 1950s that her work would never sell. When she won the Nobel Prize in 2007, she was photographed on her doorstep, returning from shopping, visibly unbothered.

15

The 1980s–2000s — Postcolonial and Postmodern
Salman Rushdie's Midnight's Children (Booker Prize 1981, Booker of Bookers 1993 and 2008) transformed British fiction — postcolonial, postmodern, mythologically rich. The Satanic Verses (1988) prompted a fatwa from Ayatollah Khomeini; Rushdie spent years in hiding. Kazuo Ishiguro (Japanese-British) — The Remains of the Day, Never Let Me Go — Nobel Prize 2017. Ian McEwan — Atonement, Saturday. Martin Amis — Money, London Fields — brought American energy to English prose. Irvine Welsh (Scottish) — Trainspotting (1993) — wrote in Edinburgh vernacular about heroin, class and Scotland's relationship with itself. Hanif Kureishi — The Buddha of Suburbia — gave voice to British Asian experience.
Salman Rushdie's fatwa was issued by Ayatollah Khomeini in February 1989. Rushdie lived under protection for nine years. He was stabbed on stage in New York in 2022, losing sight in one eye.

16

Contemporary British Literature (2000–present)
Zadie Smith's White Teeth (2000) — published when she was 24 — mapped multicultural London with exuberance and formal sophistication. Hilary Mantel's Wolf Hall trilogy (Booker Prize twice) is the greatest historical fiction in English since Scott. Ali Smith's seasonal quartet (Autumn, Winter, Spring, Summer) written and published in real time during Brexit — the most formally ambitious English fiction since Woolf. Bernardine Evaristo's Girl, Woman, Other (joint Booker 2019) gave voices to twelve Black British women across a century. Anna Burns's Milkman (Booker 2018) — set in unnamed Belfast during the Troubles, without named characters — is the most formally radical Booker winner in decades. Maggie O'Farrell, Sally Rooney (Irish), Douglas Stuart (Scottish, Booker 2020 for Shuggie Bain) — the tradition continues.
Douglas Stuart grew up in poverty in Glasgow. Shuggie Bain — about a boy caring for his alcoholic mother in 1980s Glasgow — was rejected by 32 publishers before winning the Booker Prize.

⚹

Women Writers — The Hidden Mainstream
Women have been central to British literature from the start — and repeatedly written out of it. Julian of Norwich (c.1342–1416) was the first woman known to have written a book in English — Revelations of Divine Love, a mystical theology of extraordinary sophistication. Aphra Behn (1640–1689) was the first professional woman writer in English — playwright, poet, spy and novelist (Oroonoko, 1688, one of the earliest novels in English). Jane Austen (1775–1817) — Pride and Prejudice, Sense and Sensibility, Emma, Persuasion — wrote in a household with no private space, on scraps of paper she hid under her sewing. Her six complete novels are among the most technically perfect in the language. Charlotte Brontë (1816–1855) — Jane Eyre (1847) — and her sister Emily Brontë (1818–1848) — Wuthering Heights (1847) — published originally under male pseudonyms (Currer and Ellis Bell). Elizabeth Gaskell (1810–1865) — North and South, Wives and Daughters, Mary Barton — wrote with equal power about industrial Manchester and rural England. George Eliot (Mary Ann Evans, 1819–1880) used a male pen name because she feared her work would not be taken seriously. Virginia Woolf's observation holds: 'For most of history, Anonymous was a woman.'
The Brontë sisters are perhaps the most extraordinary literary siblings in history. Charlotte, Emily and Anne all published novels in 1847. Emily died the following year, aged 30.

⚹

20th–21st Century Women Writers
Virginia Woolf (1882–1941) not only transformed the novel but wrote the most important feminist essay in English — A Room of One's Own (1929): 'A woman must have money and a room of her own if she is to write fiction.' Muriel Spark (Scottish, 1918–2006) — The Prime of Miss Jean Brodie — wit, Catholicism and menace. Doris Lessing (1919–2013) — The Golden Notebook — Nobel Prize 2007; one of the 20th century's great novels of female consciousness. Angela Carter (1940–1992) — The Bloody Chamber, Nights at the Circus — feminist Gothic, reworking fairy tales to expose their violence. Penelope Fitzgerald (1916–2000) began publishing at 60 and wrote some of the finest English prose of the century — The Bookshop, The Gate of Angels, The Blue Flower. Hilary Mantel (1952–2022) won the Booker Prize twice — the only author to do so — with Wolf Hall and Bring Up the Bodies. Zadie Smith (b.1975) — White Teeth — published at 24, mapped multicultural London with exuberance. Bernardine Evaristo (b.1959) — Girl, Woman, Other — joint Booker 2019, gave voice to twelve Black British women across a century. Ali Smith (Scottish, b.1962) — the Seasonal Quartet — the most formally ambitious English fiction since Woolf. Anna Burns (Northern Irish, b.1962) — Milkman — Booker 2018.
Penelope Fitzgerald's first novel was published when she was 60. She had been a bookseller, a BBC producer and a teacher. Her late-career novels are considered among the finest in 20th-century English literature.

🎬 Film

British cinema: from Ealing to Trainspotting, from Bond to Belfast.

UK Overview

Britain has been making films since 1895 — and has never stopped being significant.

01

British Cinema — A Complicated Identity
British cinema has always struggled with identity: is it distinct from Hollywood, or a variation of it? The answer is both. Britain produces world-class films — from Ealing comedies to Ken Loach's social realism to Christopher Nolan's blockbusters — while also serving as a production base for American studios attracted by tax credits and talent.
The UK film industry generates over £13 billion annually. Around 60% of that comes from inward investment (US studios filming in the UK).

02

The New Wave — Kitchen Sink Realism
In the late 1950s and early 1960s, British cinema broke with its class-bound traditions. Saturday Night and Sunday Morning (1960), The Loneliness of the Long Distance Runner (1962), Billy Liar (1963) — working-class protagonists, northern England, ambition thwarted by society. Angry, specific, transformative.
Saturday Night and Sunday Morning was refused a certificate by several local councils for its depiction of adultery and abortion. It made a star of Albert Finney.

03

Bond and Beyond — Genre Cinema
The James Bond franchise (started 1962) is the most successful film series in history. Carry On films defined British comedy for a generation. Hammer Horror gave British cinema a global genre identity. These popular films are as significant culturally as the art house output — they define what people worldwide associate with Britain.
The James Bond franchise has grossed over $7 billion worldwide. It has been running continuously for over 60 years.

04

The Heritage Film
Chariots of Fire (1981), A Room with a View (1985), Howards End (1992), The Remains of the Day (1993) — a genre of literary adaptations and period pieces that became both a British export success and a cliché. Beautiful, restrained, class-conscious. The international image of 'British' cinema for much of the 1980s–90s.
Chariots of Fire won Best Picture at the Oscars. Its theme tune by Vangelis is one of the most recognised pieces of film music in the world.

05

Contemporary British Cinema
Atonement, 12 Years a Slave (directed by British-born Steve McQueen), The Favourite, Belfast, Aftersun — British cinema in the 21st century is diverse, internationally recognised and formally ambitious. The BFI (British Film Institute) and Film4 have been crucial in funding distinctive work.
Steve McQueen — from London, of Grenadian and Trinidadian descent — won the Academy Award for Best Picture with 12 Years a Slave (2013).

National Contributions

From Ealing to Trainspotting, from Game of Thrones to Belfast.

🏴 England
01

Ealing Studios and British Comedy
Ealing Studios in West London produced the defining British comedies of the late 1940s and 50s: Kind Hearts and Coronets, The Lavender Hill Mob, The Ladykillers. Gentle, ironic, class-aware, with an undertone of anarchy. They established a British comic sensibility that still shapes comedy today.
Ealing Studios is the oldest continuously operating film studio in the world, founded in 1902.

02

Ken Loach — Social Realism
Ken Loach (b.1936, Warwickshire) has made films about working-class England for 60 years — Kes (1969), Raining Stones (1993), I, Daniel Blake (2016). His work is unashamedly political: the welfare system, unemployment, trade unions, poverty. He has won the Palme d'Or twice.
'Kes' — about a boy from a Yorkshire mining town who trains a kestrel — is regularly voted one of the greatest British films ever made.

03

Richard Curtis and the Rom-Com
Four Weddings and a Funeral (1994), Notting Hill (1999), Love Actually (2003) — Richard Curtis created a version of England (London, middle-class, witty, charming) that became globally dominant. Hugely influential commercially; regularly criticised for its lack of diversity and its fantasy of Englishness.
Four Weddings and a Funeral cost £3 million to make and grossed £230 million worldwide. It made Hugh Grant a global star.

04

Christopher Nolan
Nolan (b.1970, London) — Memento, The Dark Knight, Inception, Dunkirk, Oppenheimer — has become the world's most commercially successful 'serious' filmmaker. His work is intellectually ambitious and visually spectacular. Oppenheimer (2023) won seven Oscars including Best Picture.
Oppenheimer grossed over $950 million worldwide — the highest-grossing biopic in history.

🏴 Scotland
01

Trainspotting
Danny Boyle's Trainspotting (1996) — based on Irvine Welsh's novel — is the most significant Scottish film. Edinburgh heroin addicts, scabrous humour, extraordinary energy. It introduced Scotland to the world in a way that had nothing to do with kilts or heather. Its sequel T2 Trainspotting (2017) is equally accomplished.
Trainspotting cost £1.5 million and grossed £48 million. Its soundtrack — Iggy Pop, Underworld, Blur — is one of the greatest in film history.

02

Local Hero and Scottish Landscape
Bill Forsyth's Local Hero (1983) — about an American oil company executive sent to buy a Scottish village — is one of the most beloved British films. Mark Knopfler's score and the Aberdeenshire landscape are as important as the script. It captures something true about Scottish communities and their relationship to modernity.
Local Hero is regularly cited as the best Scottish film ever made. It had a budget of £3 million and is still in continuous distribution 40 years later.

03

Peter Capaldi, Peter Mullan, Peter Greenaway
Scotland's contribution to British cinema as directors, writers and actors is disproportionate. Peter Capaldi's short film Franz Kafka's It's a Wonderful Life won the Oscar. Peter Mullan's The Magdalene Sisters (2002) won the Golden Lion at Venice. Scottish actors — Sean Connery, Ewan McGregor, James McAvoy, Peter Capaldi — have defined British screen acting.
Sean Connery — from Edinburgh — was voted the greatest living Scotsman in 1999 and the greatest Scot of all time in 2004.

04

Brave, Braveheart and the Scottish Image
Hollywood has produced its own version of Scotland — Braveheart (1995, directed by Mel Gibson, filmed mostly in Ireland), Brave (2012), Highlander (1986). These films have shaped the global image of Scotland — dramatic, violent, misty — in ways that amuse and exasperate actual Scots.
Mel Gibson's Braveheart won the Academy Award for Best Picture. It contains numerous historical inaccuracies. Scots find this relationship complicated.

🏴 Wales
01

How Green Was My Valley
John Ford's How Green Was My Valley (1941) — based on Richard Llewellyn's novel about a Welsh mining family — won five Oscars including Best Picture, beating Citizen Kane. It established the image of Wales in American cinema: choral, communal, suffering, noble. Beautiful and somewhat mythologised.
How Green Was My Valley beat Citizen Kane for Best Picture. Film critics have argued about this ever since.

02

S4C and Welsh Language Cinema
S4C (the Welsh language television channel) has been a crucial funder of Welsh cinema since 1982. Hedd Wyn (1992) — about a Welsh poet killed in WWI — was the first Welsh-language film nominated for the Academy Award for Best Foreign Language Film.
Hedd Wyn tells the story of a poet whose chair at the Eisteddfod was draped in black because he had been killed before he could claim it. The Black Chair is now in the National Museum of Wales.

03

Twin Town and Contemporary Wales
Twin Town (1997) — Kevin Allen's dark comedy about twins from Swansea — is Wales's Trainspotting equivalent: anarchic, funny, deeply rooted in a specific place. Rhys Ifans made his name here. Contemporary Welsh directors — Euros Lyn, Sara Sugarman — work primarily in television.
Twin Town was filmed almost entirely in Swansea. It was made for £2.5 million and is beloved in Wales and relatively unknown elsewhere.

04

Welsh Actors — A Global Presence
Wales has produced an extraordinary number of world-class actors: Richard Burton, Anthony Hopkins, Timothy Dalton, Christian Bale, Catherine Zeta-Jones, Michael Sheen, Matthew Rhys, Luke Evans, Eve Myles. A nation of 3 million has contributed more to global screen acting than almost any comparable population.
Anthony Hopkins — from Port Talbot — won the Academy Award for Best Actor for The Silence of the Lambs (1991) and The Father (2020).

🇬🇧 Northern Ireland
01

Game of Thrones — The Belfast Effect
Game of Thrones (2011–2019) was filmed largely in Northern Ireland — the Causeway Coast, the Dark Hedges, Tollymore Forest. It brought £250 million to the economy, created a permanent studio infrastructure at Titanic Studios, and put Northern Ireland on the global film map in a way nothing else had.
The Dark Hedges — an avenue of beech trees in County Antrim used as the Kingsroad — received so many visitors after Game of Thrones that a traffic management scheme had to be introduced.

02

Belfast (Kenneth Branagh)
Kenneth Branagh's Belfast (2021) — a semi-autobiographical film about a Protestant family during the early Troubles — won the Oscar for Best Original Screenplay. It depicted the beginning of the conflict in 1969 from a child's perspective with warmth, humour and sadness.
Kenneth Branagh grew up in Belfast and moved to England as a child. Belfast was his most personal film.

03

Troubles Cinema
Films about the Troubles range from the propagandistic to the brilliant. In the Name of the Father (1993), Bloody Sunday (2002), '71 (2014), Hunger (2008, Steve McQueen's film about Bobby Sands) — these films navigated extraordinarily sensitive material with increasing sophistication.
Steve McQueen's Hunger won the Camera d'Or at Cannes in 2008. It depicts Bobby Sands's 1981 hunger strike with almost unbearable intensity.

04

Northern Irish Actors
Liam Neeson (Ballymena), Ciarán Hinds (Belfast), Jamie Dornan (Holywood, County Down), Colin Morgan (Armagh), Bronagh Gallagher (Derry) — Northern Ireland's acting talent is substantial. Liam Neeson has become one of Hollywood's most bankable stars after a career that began in Irish theatre.
Liam Neeson was cast in Schindler's List after Steven Spielberg saw him perform in a West End production.

Actresses
⚹

Northern Irish Women in Performance
Northern Ireland's women performers have made significant contributions to British and Irish screen culture. Bronagh Gallagher (b.1972, Derry) — Pulp Fiction, The Commitments, Sherlock Holmes — a Derry woman who has worked consistently across international productions. Roma Downey (b.1960, Derry) — Touched by an Angel — became one of American television's most successful performers and producers. Michelle Fairley (b.1964, Coleraine) — Catelyn Stark in Game of Thrones, and a distinguished stage career at the RSC and in the West End. Orlaith McAllister and the generation of women trained at the Lyric Theatre Belfast represent Northern Ireland's living theatrical tradition.
Michelle Fairley trained at the Royal Scottish Academy of Music and Drama. Her performance as Catelyn Stark in Game of Thrones reached an audience of over 20 million per episode.

🎨 Painting

From Turner and Constable to the YBAs and Banksy — British art and its national tributaries.

UK Overview

From Turner's light to Banksy's stencils — British painting has always had something to say.

01

The National Collections
Britain holds some of the world's greatest art collections — the National Gallery, the Tate Modern, the Tate Britain, the National Portrait Gallery, the V&A. Entry to the permanent collections of national museums is free — a policy introduced in 2001 that transformed public access to art.
The National Gallery in London holds over 2,300 paintings spanning 700 years. Free entry since 2001. It receives over 6 million visitors annually.

02

The Royal Academy and British Tradition
The Royal Academy of Arts (founded 1768) has shaped British art for 250 years — training artists, holding the Summer Exhibition (the world's largest open-submission art exhibition), and defining taste. Its presidents — Reynolds, Constable, Millais, Leighton — are a history of British art.
The Royal Academy Summer Exhibition has been held every year since 1769 — including through both World Wars.

03

British Art and Empire
British art from the 17th to the 20th century is inseparable from empire — portraits of merchants and colonisers, landscapes of colonised territories, ethnographic images of colonised peoples. The Elgin Marbles debate (should the sculptures taken from Athens be returned?) is the most visible version of a much broader reckoning.
The British Museum holds approximately 8 million objects — the largest collection in the world. The debate about repatriation involves objects from dozens of countries.

04

The Young British Artists (YBAs)
Damien Hirst, Tracey Emin, Chris Ofili, Rachel Whiteread, Gillian Wearing — the YBAs emerged in the late 1980s and made British contemporary art globally dominant for a decade. Hirst's shark in formaldehyde, Emin's unmade bed, Ofili's elephant dung paintings — provocative, commercially successful, culturally significant.
Damien Hirst's 'The Physical Impossibility of Death in the Mind of Someone Living' (the shark) was sold for £6.5 million in 2004. It has been recreated twice as the original shark decomposed.

05

Banksy and Street Art
Banksy — anonymous, Bristol-based — has made street art a globally recognised fine art form. His stencilled images appear on walls worldwide; his auction stunts (the self-shredding painting) command international attention. His identity remains unknown despite decades of speculation.
When Banksy's 'Girl with Balloon' self-shredded at Sotheby's auction in 2018, the work was renamed 'Love is in the Bin' and its value immediately doubled.

National Contributions

Each nation has a distinct visual tradition. Below, the highlights.

🏴 England
01

Hogarth — Moral Satire
William Hogarth (1697–1764) invented the narrative painting sequence — A Rake's Progress, Marriage A-la-Mode, Beer Street and Gin Lane. Savage satires of English social life: corruption, poverty, excess, hypocrisy. He created a distinctly English tradition of moral art that runs from him to Gillray to Banksy.
Hogarth's 'Gin Lane' (1751) was created to lobby Parliament for the Gin Act, which regulated gin sales. It worked.

02

Constable and Turner — The English Landscape
John Constable (1776–1837) painted Suffolk with an attention to cloud and light that transformed landscape art. J.M.W. Turner (1775–1851) took landscape into abstraction — his late paintings of light, mist and water anticipate Impressionism by 30 years. Both are in the National Gallery; both are quintessentially English.
Turner left over 30,000 works to the nation. The Tate Britain holds the Turner Bequest — the largest collection of works by any artist in a national gallery.

03

The Pre-Raphaelites
The Pre-Raphaelite Brotherhood (Millais, Rossetti, Holman Hunt) rejected academic convention and returned to medieval sources — intense colour, detailed naturalism, moral and religious subjects. Their paintings of Ophelia, Arthurian legend and social realism are among the most beloved in British art.
Millais's 'Ophelia' (1851–52) was painted by having his model Elizabeth Siddal lie in a bath of water for hours. She developed a severe chill.

04

Francis Bacon, Lucian Freud, David Hockney
Three giants of 20th-century English painting. Bacon's screaming popes and distorted figures — existential horror in paint. Freud's unflinching portraits of flesh — raw, uncomfortable, magnificent. Hockney's California swimming pools and Yorkshire landscapes — joyful, curious, technically brilliant. All three worked into their 80s.
Lucian Freud's portrait of benefits supervisor Sue Tilley sold for £17.2 million in 2008 — then the highest price for a living artist's work.

🏴 Scotland
01

The Glasgow Boys
In the 1880s, a group of Glasgow painters — James Guthrie, John Lavery, E.A. Hornel — rejected the stuffy conventions of Edinburgh's art establishment and developed a naturalistic, plein-air style influenced by French Impressionism. They became internationally recognised and put Scottish art on the European map.
The Glasgow Boys exhibited internationally throughout the 1890s. Kaiser Wilhelm II bought two of their paintings.

02

Charles Rennie Mackintosh
Mackintosh (1868–1928) was architect, designer and painter — his distinctive style fused Art Nouveau with Scottish vernacular tradition. The Glasgow School of Art (1909) is his masterpiece. His rose motif, his high-backed chairs, his watercolour flower paintings are instantly recognisable.
The Glasgow School of Art was devastated by fire twice (2014 and 2018). Restoration is ongoing. It remains Mackintosh's most important building.

03

The Scottish Colourists
S.J. Peploe, F.C.B. Cadell, J.D. Fergusson, Leslie Hunter — four painters who worked in France in the early 20th century and brought Post-Impressionist colour and boldness back to Scotland. Their still lifes, portraits and landscapes are warm, confident and immediately recognisable.
The Scottish Colourists were largely forgotten for decades, then rediscovered. Their work now commands high prices at auction.

04

Contemporary Scottish Art
Edinburgh's Scottish National Gallery of Modern Art holds a world-class collection. Artists like Peter Doig (born Edinburgh, raised Canada), Douglas Gordon (Turner Prize 1996), and Christine Borland represent Scotland's contemporary contribution. Glasgow's art school tradition continues to produce internationally significant work.
Peter Doig's paintings regularly sell for over £20 million. He is considered one of the most important figurative painters working today.

🏴 Wales
01

Richard Wilson and the Landscape Tradition
Richard Wilson (1714–1782) from Penegoes, Montgomeryshire, is considered the father of British landscape painting — he painted Welsh, English and Italian landscapes with a classical serenity that influenced Constable and Turner. He is less famous than his successors but without him they might not have existed.
Richard Wilson was one of the founders of the Royal Academy in 1768. He died in poverty, his work undervalued in his own time.

02

Kyffin Williams
Sir Kyffin Williams (1918–2006) is the most beloved Welsh painter — his dark, gestural landscapes of Snowdonia and Anglesey are unmistakably Welsh. He used a palette knife rather than a brush, creating thick, dramatic surfaces. His work hangs in Welsh homes in a way that no other artist's does.
Kyffin Williams donated over 1,000 works to Wales. He refused to move to London despite pressure to do so. He is buried on Anglesey.

03

Ceri Richards
Ceri Richards (1903–1971) from Dunvant, Swansea, is Wales's most significant modernist painter — his work engaged with Surrealism, Dylan Thomas's poetry, and Debussy's music. His 'Do Not Go Gentle' series, based on Thomas's poem, is the most powerful visual response to a Welsh literary work.
Ceri Richards represented Britain at the Venice Biennale in 1962. He is relatively unknown outside specialist art circles — an injustice.

04

The National Museum Wales
The National Museum Wales in Cardiff holds an outstanding collection of French Impressionism (Monet, Renoir, Cézanne) as well as the national art collection. Its collection of Welsh art is comprehensive. Entry is free.
The National Museum Wales holds works by Monet, Renoir, Cézanne, Pissarro and Sisley given to the nation by the Davies sisters of Llandinam in 1951 — one of the most important art bequests in British history.

🇬🇧 Northern Ireland
01

Paul Henry — Irish Landscape
Paul Henry (1877–1958) from Belfast is Northern Ireland's most recognised painter — his images of Connemara's white cottages, turf stacks and dramatic skies defined the visual image of the west of Ireland for a generation. His work was used on railway posters; it shaped how Ireland presented itself to the world.
Paul Henry's images were used extensively by the Great Southern Railways to promote tourism in the west of Ireland in the 1920s.

02

The Ulster Arts Scene
Belfast's arts scene has grown dramatically since the peace process. The MAC (Metropolitan Arts Centre) opened in 2012. The Golden Thread Gallery and Context Gallery support contemporary work. The annual Belfast Photo Festival is internationally significant. Northern Ireland's arts funding has increased substantially since the GFA.
The MAC (Metropolitan Arts Centre) hosts over 120 events per year and has become Belfast's most significant cultural building.

03

Colin Davidson
Colin Davidson (b.1968) from Belfast is Northern Ireland's most significant contemporary painter — large-scale, hyper-realist portraits of faces in moments of vulnerability. His subjects have included Seamus Heaney, Van Morrison and, controversially, victims and perpetrators of the Troubles in the same exhibition.
Colin Davidson's portrait of Seamus Heaney was selected as a postage stamp by An Post (Irish postal service) after Heaney's death in 2013.

04

F.E. McWilliam
F.E. McWilliam (1909–1992) from Banbridge, County Down, is Northern Ireland's most important sculptor — his 'Women of Belfast' series (1972–1973), made in response to a bombing, are stark, fragmented figures of extraordinary emotional power. He is relatively little known outside specialist circles.
The F.E. McWilliam Gallery in Banbridge is the only gallery in Ireland dedicated to a single artist. His 'Women of Belfast' series is considered one of the most important responses to the Troubles in any art form.

🏛 Architecture

Stone, slate, glass and concrete — the built environment as cultural history.

UK Overview

Britain's buildings tell its history more honestly than its history books.

01

A Layered History in Stone
British architecture is a palimpsest — layer upon layer of history visible in a single city street. Roman walls, Norman castles, medieval churches, Georgian terraces, Victorian warehouses, Brutalist towers, contemporary glass. Britain tears down less than most comparable countries and has consequently preserved more.
England has over 375,000 listed buildings — structures of special architectural or historic interest protected by law. Scotland has over 47,000.

02

The Georgian City
The 18th century produced Britain's most coherent urban architecture — the Georgian terrace. Bath (a UNESCO World Heritage Site), Edinburgh's New Town, London's Bloomsbury and Belgravia, Dublin (just across the water) — these cities of honey-coloured stone and elegant proportions remain the template for 'beautiful city' worldwide.
Bath's Royal Crescent (1767–1774) — a sweeping crescent of 30 terraced houses — is one of the finest examples of Georgian architecture anywhere. It has been used as a film location hundreds of times.

03

Victorian Ambition
The Victorians built with extraordinary ambition — the Houses of Parliament (Gothic Revival, 1840–1870), St Pancras Station (1868), the Natural History Museum (1881), Tower Bridge (1894). An age of industrial confidence expressed in stone, brick and iron. The Victorian city is the backdrop of most British urban life.
The Houses of Parliament took 30 years to build. Its architect, Augustus Pugin, went mad before it was completed, partly from the strain of designing every detail — down to the inkwells.

04

Brutalism — Love It or Hate It
Post-war Britain built in concrete — the Barbican in London, the National Theatre, Park Hill in Sheffield, Trellick Tower. Brutalism intended to provide dignified social housing and public buildings; in practice results varied enormously. Some is magnificent; some is genuinely oppressive. Demolition debates are constant.
The Barbican in London — a Brutalist residential complex completed in 1982 — is now one of the most sought-after addresses in the city. Its arts centre is one of Europe's largest.

05

Contemporary British Architecture
Norman Foster, Richard Rogers, Zaha Hadid (Iraqi-born, British-based), David Chipperfield — British architecture has been globally dominant since the 1980s. The Gherkin, the Shard, the Lloyd's Building, the Millennium Dome. The Pritzker Prize (architecture's Nobel) has been won by British architects six times.
Zaha Hadid — the first woman to win the Pritzker Prize (2004) — studied at the Architectural Association in London and based her practice there throughout her career.

National Contributions

From Stonehenge to the Senedd, from Edinburgh's New Town to Belfast's peace walls.

🏴 England
01

Stonehenge and Prehistoric England
Stonehenge (c.3000–1500 BC) remains the most mysterious building in Britain — a monument whose purpose is still debated, whose construction required technologies and social organisation we struggle to explain. It is aligned with the summer solstice. It attracts 1.5 million visitors a year.
The largest stones at Stonehenge weigh 25 tonnes and were transported from Wales — 150 miles away — by methods that remain disputed.

02

The Cathedral Tradition
England's medieval cathedrals are among the great buildings of the world — Durham (Norman, begun 1093), Salisbury (Gothic, begun 1220), Winchester, Canterbury, York Minster. Built over centuries, combining multiple styles, they are the physical expression of medieval theology and civic pride.
Durham Cathedral has been described as 'one of the great architectural experiences of Europe.' It was built in just 40 years — extraordinarily fast for its scale.

03

Christopher Wren and the City of London
After the Great Fire of London (1666), Christopher Wren rebuilt 51 churches and St Paul's Cathedral. His domed St Paul's defined the London skyline for 300 years. Wren also designed the Royal Naval College at Greenwich, the Royal Hospital Chelsea, and the Monument to the Great Fire.
Wren's epitaph in St Paul's (which he designed): 'Si monumentum requiris, circumspice' — If you seek his monument, look around you.

04

The Country House
The English country house — Blenheim, Chatsworth, Longleat, Castle Howard — is a distinctive building type and a social institution. Built by the aristocracy from the 17th century onwards, they are now managed by English Heritage, the National Trust, or as tourist attractions. Their gardens are as important as the buildings.
Blenheim Palace — birthplace of Winston Churchill — was given to the Duke of Marlborough by a grateful nation after his victory at the Battle of Blenheim in 1704.

🏴 Scotland
01

Scottish Baronial Style
The distinctive style of Scottish castle architecture — harled walls, crow-stepped gables, conical turrets — developed from the 15th century and was revived by the Victorians as 'Scottish Baronial.' Balmoral (Queen Victoria's Scottish home, rebuilt 1853) is its most famous example; its influence spread through Scottish domestic architecture.
Queen Victoria was responsible for the Victorian romanticisation of the Highlands — and, through Balmoral, for establishing Scottish Baronial as the style of Scottish aspiration.

02

Edinburgh's Dual City
Edinburgh is architecturally two cities: the medieval Old Town (organic, vertical, built on a ridge, a UNESCO World Heritage Site) and the Georgian New Town (planned, horizontal, elegant). The two face each other across Princes Street Gardens. Together they constitute one of the finest urban environments in the world.
Edinburgh's New Town was built over 70 years (1767–1850) to a plan by 22-year-old James Craig, who won a competition. It is the most complete example of Georgian town planning in the world.

03

Charles Rennie Mackintosh
Mackintosh (1868–1928) transformed architecture, interior design and decorative arts. The Glasgow School of Art (1897–1909), the Hill House in Helensburgh, the Willow Tea Rooms — each integrating architecture, furniture, textiles and decoration into a total work of art. His influence was greater on the Continent than in Britain during his lifetime.
The Vienna Secessionists — Klimt, Schiele, Hoffmann — were deeply influenced by Mackintosh. He was celebrated in Vienna years before he was fully recognised in Scotland.

04

Tenements — The Scottish Urban Form
The Glasgow and Edinburgh tenement — a stone building of 4–6 storeys, with multiple self-contained flats sharing a common stair — is Scotland's distinctive urban housing type. Derided as slums in the mid-20th century; now recognised as excellent urban housing. Dense, sociable, durable.
Glasgow's tenements were built to last — many are now 150 years old and in excellent condition. The worst were demolished in the 1960s and replaced with tower blocks that have since been demolished.

🏴 Wales
01

Edward I's Iron Ring of Castles
After conquering Wales in 1282, Edward I of England built a series of castles to control it — Caernarfon, Conwy, Harlech, Beaumaris, Rhuddlan. Designed by Master James of St George (from Savoy), they are considered the finest examples of medieval military architecture in Europe.
Edward I's Welsh castles are collectively a UNESCO World Heritage Site. Beaumaris — the last and most perfect — was never actually finished. It has been described as 'the most technically perfect castle in Britain.'

02

Welsh Vernacular Architecture
Wales has a distinctive vernacular building tradition — whitewashed longhouses, farmhouses built with the cattle under the same roof, the ubiquitous grey slate of the North. The hill farmhouse of Snowdonia, low and grey, growing from the landscape, is as distinctively Welsh as the language.
The Welsh longhouse — house and byre under one roof — was the standard dwelling for Welsh hill farmers until the 19th century. Some are still inhabited.

03

Cardiff's Civic Architecture
Cardiff became Wales's capital in 1955 and has since invested significantly in civic architecture. The Senedd building (Richard Rogers, 2006) — transparent, democratic in its symbolism — and Wales Millennium Centre (Jonathan Adams, 2004) — clad in steel with text cut into the facade — are both outstanding 21st-century buildings.
The words cut into the Wales Millennium Centre facade are in Welsh and English: 'In These Stones Horizons Sing' / 'Creu Gwir fel Gwydr o Ffwrnais Awen.' They were written by the building's architect.

04

The Industrial Sublime
Wales's industrial architecture — the slate quarries of Dinorwig and Penrhyn, the ironworks of Blaenavon (now a UNESCO World Heritage Site), the colliery winding houses of the Valleys — is as significant as its medieval castles. These structures record a different kind of Welsh achievement.
Blaenavon ironworks (1788) is one of the best-preserved ironworks of the early industrial revolution. It was designated a UNESCO World Heritage Site in 2000.

🇬🇧 Northern Ireland
01

The Plantation Architecture
The Plantation of Ulster in the 17th century left a distinctive architectural legacy — the 'Planter's Gothic' churches built by settlers, the fortified houses ('planter's houses') built as secure residences in hostile territory, and the planned towns (Londonderry's walled city is the best example).
Derry/Londonderry is the only walled city in Ireland and one of the best-preserved walled cities in Europe. Its walls were built between 1613 and 1619 and have never been breached.

02

The Victorian Industrial City
Belfast grew from a market town of 20,000 in 1800 to a city of 400,000 in 1900 — one of the fastest urban expansions in history. Its Victorian architecture reflects that confidence: City Hall (1906), the Crown Liquor Saloon (1885), the Grand Opera House (1895), and the massive shipyard infrastructure of Harland and Wolff.
Belfast's Crown Liquor Saloon is owned by the National Trust — the only pub in the National Trust's portfolio. Its Victorian interior is extraordinarily preserved.

03

Titanic Belfast
The Titanic Belfast visitor centre (2012, Civic Arts / Eric R. Kuhne) is Northern Ireland's most significant contemporary building — its angular facades reference the hull of a ship, clad in geometric aluminium panels. It sits at the Thompson Graving Dock where the Titanic was fitted out. It has received over 6 million visitors.
Titanic Belfast was voted the World's Leading Tourist Attraction at the World Travel Awards in 2016.

04

The Peace Walls as Architecture
Northern Ireland's peace walls — barriers between Protestant and Catholic communities in Belfast, Derry and elsewhere — are a form of defensive architecture unlike anything else in Western Europe. Over 90 walls still stand. They are simultaneously scars on the city and, increasingly, canvases for murals and art.
The largest peace wall in Belfast — the Peace Line on Cupar Way — is over 5 metres high and over 1 kilometre long. It was built in 1969 and has been extended several times since.

⚽ Sport

Sport as identity, community and cultural export — what each nation plays, and why it matters.

01

Gaelic Football and Hurling
Northern Ireland contains both the GAA (Gaelic Athletic Association) tradition — Gaelic football and hurling, largely Catholic/nationalist communities — and football (soccer) and rugby union, more associated with Protestant/unionist communities. The GAA was founded in 1884 partly as a cultural nationalist movement. Its games are played at county level; the All-Ireland finals at Croke Park in Dublin draw 80,000.
Until 2005, members of the British Army and Royal Ulster Constabulary were banned from playing GAA sports — a rule known as Rule 21. Its removal was a significant gesture toward reconciliation.

02

Football — Two Associations
Northern Ireland has its own football association (Irish FA, founded 1880 — the fourth oldest in the world) separate from the Republic's FAI. The national team plays in green and has produced world-class players: George Best, Pat Jennings, Martin O'Neill, David Healy, Steven Davis. Qualifying for the 2016 European Championship was a historic achievement.
Northern Ireland qualified for the 1982 and 1986 World Cups. In 1982, they beat Spain in Valencia — one of the great upsets in World Cup history.

03

George Best
George Best (1946–2005) from the Cregagh estate in east Belfast is the most gifted footballer Britain has produced — and, many argue, the most gifted in history. With Manchester United in the 1960s he was extraordinary: two-footed, fast, creative, brave. He was European Footballer of the Year in 1968. His alcoholism ended his career too early and his life too soon.
George Best's passport listed his occupation as 'footballer.' When asked where all his money went, he said: 'I spent a lot of money on booze, birds and fast cars. The rest I just squandered.'

04

Rugby Union
Ulster Rugby — Northern Ireland's provincial team — plays at Kingspan Stadium in Belfast and has been one of the most successful teams in European rugby, winning the Heineken Cup in 1999. Northern Ireland contributes significantly to the Ireland rugby team (which, uniquely, represents the whole island). The cross-border team is a model of sport transcending politics.
The Ireland rugby team is the only cross-border sporting team representing the whole island of Ireland. It has been operating since 1875 — through partition, the Troubles and Brexit — without interruption.

05

Golf
Northern Ireland has produced three of the greatest golfers of the modern era: Rory McIlroy (four major championships), Darren Clarke (Open Championship 2011), and Graeme McDowell (US Open 2010). For a population of 1.9 million to produce three major champions simultaneously is statistically extraordinary.
Rory McIlroy — from Holywood, County Down — won four major championships before the age of 26. He is considered the best golfer of his generation.

06

Horse Racing
Horse racing is deeply embedded in Northern Ireland's culture — particularly in the rural Protestant farming community. The Down Royal racecourse (County Down) is one of Ireland's oldest, founded in 1685. The sport crosses community boundaries more successfully than most in Northern Ireland.
Down Royal is the only racecourse in Northern Ireland. It hosts the Ulster Derby and is one of the oldest racecourses in Ireland.

🎭 Comedy & Pop Culture

Humour, entertainment, fashion and popular culture — the side of each nation that makes the world laugh (or cringe).

01

Derry Girls (2018–2022)
Lisa McGee's Derry Girls (Channel 4, 2018–2022) is the finest comedy about the Troubles ever made — and one of the greatest British sitcoms of the 21st century. Set in 1990s Derry during the final years of the conflict, it follows five teenagers (four girls and 'the wee English fella') navigating adolescence against a backdrop of bombs, checkpoints and ceasefire negotiations. It managed to be genuinely funny about something genuinely terrible.
Derry Girls was watched by a quarter of Northern Ireland's entire population when it aired — the most-watched programme in Northern Ireland's television history. Series 3's final episode, set on the day of the Good Friday Agreement referendum, was described as 'the greatest television finale of the decade.'

02

The Derry Girls Effect
Derry Girls transformed how Northern Ireland was perceived — internationally and by itself. The show depicted a community that laughed, fought, loved and got on with life despite the Troubles — rather than being defined entirely by the conflict. Tourism to Derry increased dramatically. A Derry Girls mural in the city became one of Northern Ireland's most photographed locations.
The Derry Girls mural on Orchard Street in Derry shows the five main characters. It was painted in 2019 and has been visited by hundreds of thousands of tourists, including the cast and the US President.

03

Father Ted and Irish Comedy
Father Ted (Channel 4, 1995–1998) — written by Graham Linehan and Arthur Mathews, set on a fictional Irish island — is one of the most beloved British sitcoms ever made. Though set in the Republic, it draws on an all-island Catholic culture that Northern Ireland shares. 'That money was just resting in my account.' Its influence on subsequent British comedy is enormous.
Father Ted won the BAFTA for Best Sitcom in 1998. Graham Linehan has since become a controversial figure for reasons unrelated to his comedy writing.

04

Northern Irish Humour
Northern Irish humour is distinctive — warm, self-deprecating, relying heavily on 'slagging' (affectionate mockery) and understatement about genuinely difficult things. The ability to find comedy in the Troubles — carefully, from the inside — is part of how communities processed trauma. Frank Carson (1926–2012, Belfast) was one of Britain's best-loved comedians: 'It's the way I tell 'em.'
Frank Carson appeared on every major British television variety show from the 1970s to the 2000s. His catchphrase 'It's the way I tell 'em' became one of the most recognised in British comedy.

👑 Historical Figures

The people who shaped each nation — monarchs, rebels, scientists, reformers, artists, visionaries.

01

Saint Patrick (c.385–461)
Romano-British Christian enslaved in Ireland as a teenager. Escaped, returned as a missionary and converted much of Ireland to Christianity. His two surviving writings are the oldest documents written in Ireland.
Patrick's last message: 'I arise today through the strength of heaven' — the Breastplate of St Patrick.

02

Grace O'Malley (c.1530–c.1603)
Irish pirate queen, chieftain of the Ó Máille clan. Commanded fleets, led raids, met Elizabeth I face-to-face (they spoke in Latin — neither spoke the other's language). Defied English colonial authority for decades.
Grace O'Malley refused to bow to Elizabeth I — arguing she was a queen in her own right and owed no feudal allegiance.

03

Brian Boru (941–1014)
High King of Ireland who defeated the Viking alliance at the Battle of Clontarf (1014), dying in his tent after the victory. Ireland's greatest king.
Brian Boru was 73 when he led his army at Clontarf. He was killed by a fleeing Viking after the battle was won.

04

Wolfe Tone (1763–1798)
Protestant lawyer who founded the United Irishmen (1791), seeking an Irish republic uniting Catholics and Protestants. Led the 1798 Rebellion with French support. Died in prison at 35. The founding father of Irish republicanism.
Wolfe Tone's vision of a non-sectarian Irish republic is cited by both sides of the Northern Ireland divide.

05

Mary Ann McCracken (1770–1866)
Belfast linen merchant, abolitionist, United Irishwoman, philanthropist. Campaigned for Catholic emancipation, abolition of slavery and women's rights into her 90s. Belfast's greatest citizen.
Mary Ann McCracken was still collecting fares as a charity worker on Belfast's wharves in her final years. She lived to 96.

06

Bernadette Devlin McAliskey (b.1947)
Elected to Westminster in 1969 at 21 — the youngest woman ever elected to the British Parliament at the time. Civil rights activist who famously punched the British Home Secretary in the House of Commons after Bloody Sunday.
Bernadette Devlin delivered the key to New York City — given to her by Mayor Lindsay — to the Black Panthers, saying it belonged to the oppressed, not their oppressors.

07

Seamus Heaney (1939–2013)
From Bellaghy, County Derry. Nobel Prize in Literature 1995. The most celebrated poet from the island of Ireland since Yeats. His last message, sent minutes before his death: 'Noli timere' — Do not be afraid.
Heaney's death prompted grief across Ireland that transcended political and community divisions.

08

George Best (1946–2005)
From the Cregagh estate in east Belfast. The most gifted footballer Britain has produced. European Footballer of the Year 1968. His alcoholism ended his career too early. 'I spent a lot of money on booze, birds and fast cars. The rest I just squandered.'
Manchester United's Belfast scout sent a telegram to Matt Busby that read: 'I think I've found you a genius.' Best was 15.

09

Mo Mowlam (1949–2005)
Secretary of State for Northern Ireland who negotiated the Good Friday Agreement. Visited loyalist prisoners in the Maze Prison — a move colleagues thought reckless. It helped save the peace process. She was battling a brain tumour throughout.
Mo Mowlam was consistently rated the most popular British politician of her generation.

10

C.S. Lewis (1898–1963)
From Belfast. Oxford and Cambridge academic. Author of the Narnia chronicles — The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe has sold over 100 million copies. The Narnia landscape was inspired by the Mourne Mountains of County Down.
Lewis and J.R.R. Tolkien were close friends at Oxford. Tolkien's encouragement helped convince Lewis to return to Christianity.
